<p>It is worth noting that, in looking back in 2013 on the agency he helped shape from the beginning, Administrator William Ruckelshaus observed that a danger for EPA was that air, water, waste and other programs would be unconnected, placed in “silos,” a problem that persists more than 50 years later, albeit less so than at the start.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="EPA_Safer_Choice">EPA Safer Choice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: EPA Safer Choice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Choice" title="EPA Safer Choice">EPA Safer Choice</a> label, previously known as the Design for the Environment (DfE) label, helps consumers and commercial buyers identify and select products with safer chemical ingredients, without sacrificing quality or performance. When a product has the Safer Choice label, it means that every intentionally-added ingredient in the product has been evaluated by EPA scientists. Only the safest possible functional ingredients are allowed in products with the Safer Choice label.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safer_Detergents_Stewardship_Initiative">Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Through the <a href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Detergents_Stewardship_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="EPA Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative">Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative</a> (SDSI),<sup id="cite_ref-sdsi_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sdsi-35">[35]</a></sup> EPA's Design for the Environment (DfE) recognizes environmental leaders who voluntarily commit to the use of safer <a href="/wiki/Surfactants" class="mw-redirect" title="Surfactants">surfactants</a>. Safer surfactants are the ones that break down quickly to non-polluting compounds and help protect aquatic life in both fresh and salt water. <a href="/wiki/Nonylphenol_ethoxylates" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonylphenol ethoxylates">Nonylphenol ethoxylates</a>, commonly referred to as NPEs, are an example of a surfactant class that does not meet the definition of a safer surfactant.</p>
<p>The Design for the Environment, which was renamed to <a href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Choice" title="EPA Safer Choice">EPA Safer Choice</a> in 2015, has identified safer alternative surfactants through partnerships with industry and environmental advocates. These safer alternatives are comparable in cost and are readily available. CleanGredients<sup id="cite_ref-cleangredients_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cleangredients-36">[36]</a></sup> is a source of safer surfactants.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_Star">Energy Star</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Energy Star">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1992 the EPA launched the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Star" title="Energy Star">Energy Star</a> program, a voluntary program that fosters energy efficiency. As of 2006, more than 40,000 Energy Star products were available including major appliances, office equipment, lighting, home electronics, and more. In addition, the label can also be found on new homes and commercial and industrial buildings. In 2006, about 12 percent of new housing in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> was labeled Energy Star.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA2006_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2006-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EPA estimates it saved about $14 billion in energy costs in 2006 alone. The Energy Star program has helped spread the use of <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">LED</a> <a href="/wiki/Traffic_light" title="Traffic light">traffic lights</a>, efficient <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent light">fluorescent lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_management" title="Power management">power management</a> systems for office equipment, and low <a href="/wiki/Standby_power" title="Standby power">standby</a> energy use.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SmartGrowth">SmartGrowth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: SmartGrowth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA's Smart Growth Program begaun in 1998, is to help communities improve their development practices and get the type of development they want. Together with local, state, and national experts EPA encourage development strategies that protect human health and the environment, create economic opportunities, and provide attractive and affordable neighborhoods for people of all income levels.<sup id="cite_ref-epa.gov_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pesticides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA administers the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Insecticide,_Fungicide,_and_Rodenticide_Act" title="Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act">Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act</a> (FIFRA) (which is much older than the agency) and registers all <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> legally sold in the United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_economy">Fuel economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fuel economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Manufacturers selling <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobiles</a> in the United States are required to provide EPA <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> test results for their vehicles and the manufacturers are not allowed to provide results from alternate sources.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The fuel economy is calculated using the emissions data collected during two of the vehicle's <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(1970)" class="mw-redirect" title="Clean Air Act (1970)">Clean Air Act</a> certification tests by measuring the total volume of carbon captured from the exhaust during the tests.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The testing system was originally developed in 1972 and used <a href="/wiki/Driving_cycle" title="Driving cycle">driving cycles</a> designed to simulate driving during rush-hour in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> during that era. Until 1984 the EPA reported the exact fuel economy figures calculated from the test.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1984, the EPA began adjusting city (aka <a href="/wiki/UDDS" title="UDDS">Urban Dynamometer Driving Schedule</a> or <a href="/wiki/UDDS" title="UDDS">UDDS</a>) results downward by 10% and highway (aka HighWay Fuel Economy Test or HWFET) results by 22% to compensate for changes in driving conditions since 1972, and to better correlate the EPA test results with real-world driving. In 1996, the EPA proposed updating the Federal Testing Procedures<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> to add a new higher-speed test (US06) and an air-conditioner-on test (SC03) to further improve the correlation of fuel economy and emission estimates with real-world reports. In December 2006 the updated testing methodology was finalized to be implemented in model year 2008 vehicles and set the precedent of a 12-year review cycle for the test procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-epafe_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epafe-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2005, EPA launched a program called "Your MPG" that allows drivers to add real-world fuel economy statistics into a database on the EPA's fuel economy website and compare them with others and with the original EPA test results.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EPA conducts fuel economy tests on very few vehicles. "Just 18 of the EPA's 17,000 employees work in the automobile-testing department in Ann Arbor, Michigan, examining 200 to 250 vehicles a year, or roughly 15 percent of new models. As to that other 85 percent, the EPA takes automakers at their word—without any testing-accepting submitted results as accurate."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Two-thirds of the vehicles the EPA tests themselves are randomly selected and the remaining third is tested for specific reasons.</p>
<p>Although originally created as a reference point for fossil-fueled vehicles, <a href="/wiki/Driving_cycle" title="Driving cycle">driving cycles</a> have been used for estimating how many miles an electric vehicle will get on a single charge.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_quality">Air quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Air quality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Air Quality Modeling Group (AQMG) is in the EPA's Office of Air and Radiation (OAR) and leads in the full range of <a href="/wiki/Air_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Air quality">air quality</a> models, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_dispersion_modeling" title="Atmospheric dispersion modeling">atmospheric dispersion modeling</a> and other mathematical simulation techniques used in assessing the impacts of <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> sources and control strategies. It serves other EPA headquarters staff, EPA regional Offices, and State and local environmental agencies, coordinates with the EPA's Office of Research and Development on the development of new models and techniques, and wider issues of atmospheric research and conducts modeling analyses to support policy and regulatory decisions of the EPA's Office of Air Quality Planning and Standards (OAQPS). It is located in <a href="/wiki/Research_Triangle_Park" title="Research Triangle Park">Research Triangle Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. Controlling air pollution helps diminish the risk of <a href="/wiki/List_of_pollution-related_diseases" title="List of pollution-related diseases">pollution-related diseases</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_spill_prevention_program">Oil spill prevention program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Oil spill prevention program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA’s oil spill prevention program includes the Spill Prevention, Control, and Countermeasure (SPCC) and the Facility Response Plan (FRP) rules. The SPCC Rule applies to all facilities that store, handle, process, gather, transfer, refine, distribute, use or consume oil or oil products. Oil products includes petroleum and non-petroleum oils as well as: animal fats, oils and greases; fish and marine mammal oils; and vegetable oils. It mandates a written plan for facilities that store more than 1,320 gallons of fuel above ground or more than 42,000 gallons below-ground, and which might discharge to navigable waters (as defined in the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>) or adjoining shorelines. <a href="/wiki/Secondary_spill_containment" title="Secondary spill containment">Secondary spill containment</a> is mandated at oil storage facilities and oil release containment is required at oil development sites.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxics_Release_Inventory">Toxics Release Inventory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Toxics Release Inventory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Toxics_Release_Inventory" title="Toxics Release Inventory">Toxics Release Inventory</a> (TRI) is a resource for learning about toxic chemical releases and pollution prevention activities reported by industrial and federal facilities. TRI data support informed decision-making by communities, government agencies, companies, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WaterSense">WaterSense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: WaterSense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/WaterSense" class="mw-redirect" title="WaterSense">WaterSense</a> is an EPA program launched in June 2006 to encourage <a href="/wiki/Water_efficiency" title="Water efficiency">water efficiency</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> through the use of a special <a href="/wiki/Label" title="Label">label</a> on <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumer</a> <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">products</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Timeline_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-47">[47]</a></sup> Products include high-efficiency toilets (HETs), bathroom sink faucets (and accessories), and irrigation equipment. WaterSense is a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/voluntary" class="extiw" title="wikt:voluntary">voluntary</a> program, with EPA developing specifications for water-efficient products through a public process and product testing by independent laboratories.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drinking_water">Drinking water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Drinking water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA ensures safe <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> for the public, by setting standards for more than 160,000 <a href="/wiki/Public_water_system" title="Public water system">public water systems</a> nationwide. EPA oversees states, local governments and water suppliers to enforce the standards under the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a>. The program includes regulation of <a href="/wiki/Injection_well" title="Injection well">injection wells</a> in order to protect underground sources of drinking water. Select readings of amounts of certain contaminants in drinking water, precipitation, and surface water, in addition to milk and air, are reported on EPA's Rad Net web site<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> in a section entitled Envirofacts<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>. Despite mandatory reporting certain readings exceeding EPA MCL levels may be deleted or not included.<sup id="cite_ref-Bauer2012B_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2012B-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RadNet2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadNet2-52">[52]</a></sup> In 2013, an EPA draft revision relaxed regulations for radiation exposure through drinking water, stating that current standards are impractical to enforce. The EPA recommended that intervention was not necessary until drinking water was contaminated with radioactive iodine 131 at a concentration of 81,000 picocuries per liter (the limit for short term exposure set by the International Atomic Energy Agency), which was 27,000 times the prior EPA limit of 3 picocuries per liter for long term exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_relaxes_regulations_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_relaxes_regulations-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Pollutant_Discharge_Elimination_System">National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Pollutant_Discharge_Elimination_System" class="mw-redirect" title="National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System">National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System</a> (NPDES) permit program addresses water pollution by regulating <a href="/wiki/Point_source" title="Point source">point sources</a> which discharge pollutants to US waters. Created in 1972 by the Clean Water Act, the NPDES permit program is authorized to state governments to perform its many permitting, administrative, and enforcement aspects.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_protection">Radiation protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Radiation protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA has the following seven project groups to protect the public from radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Radioactive Waste Management<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>Emergency Preparedness and Response Programs<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Protective Action Guides And Planning Guidance for Radiological Incidents: EPA developed a manual as guideline for local and state governments to protect the public from a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> the 2017 version being a 15 year update.</li>
<li>EPA’s Role in Emergency Response - Special Teams<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li>Technologically Enhanced Naturally Occurring Radioactive Materials (TENORM) Program<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li>Radiation Standards for Air and Drinking Water Programs<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>Federal Guidance for Radiation Protection<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools_for_Schools">Tools for Schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tools for Schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA's Indoor Air Quality Tools for Schools Program helps schools to maintain a healthy environment and reduce exposures to indoor environmental contaminants. It helps school personnel identify, solve, and prevent indoor air quality problems in the school environment. Through the use of a multi-step management plan and checklists for the entire building, schools can lower their students' and staff's risk of exposure to asthma triggers.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_Education">Environmental Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Environmental Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Education_Act" title="National Environmental Education Act">National Environmental Education Act</a> of 1990 requires EPA to provide national leadership to increase environmental literacy. EPA established the Office of Environmental Education to implement this program.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_Impact_Statement_Reviews">Environmental Impact Statement Reviews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Environmental Impact Statement Reviews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA is responsible for reviewing <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Impact_Statement" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental Impact Statement">Environmental Impact Statements</a> of other federal agencies' projects, under the <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act</a> (NEPA).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clean_School_Bus_USA">Clean School Bus USA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Clean School Bus USA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Clean School Bus USA is a national partnership to reduce children's exposure to diesel exhaust by eliminating unnecessary school bus idling, installing effective emission control systems on newer buses and replacing the oldest buses in the fleet with newer ones. Its goal is to reduce both children's exposure to diesel exhaust and the amount of air pollution created by diesel school buses.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_justice">Environmental justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Environmental justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The EPA has been criticized for its lack of progress towards <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a>. Administrator <a href="/wiki/Christine_Todd_Whitman" title="Christine Todd Whitman">Christine Todd Whitman</a> was criticized for her changes to President Bill Clinton's <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)">Executive Order</a> 12898 during 2001, removing the requirements for government agencies to take the poor and minority populations into special consideration when making changes to environmental legislation, and therefore defeating the spirit of the Executive Order.<sup id="cite_ref-O.27Neil_report_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Neil_report-66">[66]</a></sup> In a March 2004 report, the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Inspector_General" class="mw-redirect" title="Office of the Inspector General">inspector general</a> of the agency concluded that the EPA "has not developed a clear vision or a comprehensive strategic plan, and has not established values, goals, expectations, and performance measurements" for environmental justice in its daily operations. Another report in September 2006 found the agency still had failed to review the success of its programs, policies and activities towards environmental justice.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Studies have also found that poor and minority populations were underserved by the EPA's <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> program, and that this situation was worsening.<sup id="cite_ref-O.27Neil_report_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Neil_report-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barriers_to_enforcing_environmental_justice">Barriers to enforcing environmental justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Barriers to enforcing environmental justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Many environmental justice issues are <a href="/wiki/Localization" class="mw-disambig" title="Localization">localized</a>, and therefore difficult to address by a federal agency, such as the EPA. Without strong media attention, political interest, or 'crisis' status, local issues are less likely to be addressed at the federal level compared to larger, well publicized incidents.</p>
<p>Conflicting political powers in successive administrations: The White House maintains direct control over the EPA, and its enforcements are subject to the political agenda of who is in power. Republicans and Democrats differ in their approaches to environmental justice. While President Bill Clinton signed the executive order 12898, the Bush administration did not develop a clear plan or establish goals for integrating environmental justice into everyday practices, affecting the motivation for environmental enforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The EPA is responsible for preventing and detecting environmental crimes, informing the public of environmental enforcement, and setting and monitoring standards of air pollution, water pollution, hazardous wastes and chemicals. "It is difficult to construct a specific mission statement given its wide range of responsibilities."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is impossible to address every environmental crime adequately or efficiently if there is no specific mission statement to refer to. The EPA answers to various groups, competes for resources, and confronts a wide array of harms to the environment. All of these present challenges, including a lack of resources, its self-policing policy, and a broadly defined legislation that creates too much discretion for EPA officers.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The EPA "does not have the authority or resources to address injustices without an increase in federal mandates" requiring private industries to consider the environmental ramifications of their activities.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_vessel">Research vessel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Research vessel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/USNS_Bold_(T-AGOS-12)" title="USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)">USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OSV_Bold.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/220px-OSV_Bold.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/330px-OSV_Bold.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/440px-OSV_Bold.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OSV_Bold.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
OSV <i>Bold</i> docked at Port Canaveral, FL</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 3, 2004, the United States Navy transferred <a href="/wiki/USNS_Bold_(T-AGOS-12)" title="USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)">USNS <i>Bold</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_class_ocean_surveillance_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Stalwart class ocean surveillance ship"><i>Stalwart</i> class ocean surveillance ship</a>, to the EPA, now known as OSV <i>Bold</i>. The ship, previously used in anti-submarine operations during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, is equipped with sidescan sonar, underwater video, water and sediment sampling instruments, used in study of ocean and coastline. One of the major missions of the <i>Bold</i> was to monitor for ecological impact sites where materials are dumped from dredging operations in U.S. ports.<sup id="cite_ref-bold_brochure_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold_brochure-72">[72]</a></sup> In 2013, the <i>Bold</i> was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Central_Community_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Seattle Central Community College">Seattle Central Community College</a> (SCCC) by the <a href="/wiki/General_Services_Administration" title="General Services Administration">General Services Administration</a>. SCCC demonstrated in a competition that they would put it to the highest and best purpose, and acquired the ship at a cost of $5,000.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_identification">Advance identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Advance identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Advance identification, or ADID, is a planning process used by the EPA to identify wetlands and other bodies of water and their respective suitability for the discharge of dredged and fill material. The EPA conducts the process in cooperation with the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">Army Corps of Engineers</a> and local states or <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American Tribes</a>. As of February 1993, 38 ADID projects had been completed and 33 were ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_Information_Act_processing_performance">Freedom of Information Act processing performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Freedom of Information Act processing performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the latest <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Effective_Government" title="Center for Effective Government">Center for Effective Government</a> analysis of 15 federal agencies which receive the most <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a> FOIA requests, published in 2015 (using 2012 and 2013 data, the most recent years available), the EPA earned a D by scoring 67 out of a possible 100 points, i.e. did not earn a satisfactory overall grade.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_.281983-present.29">Controversies (1983-present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Controversies (1983-present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Epaheadquarters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/220px-Epaheadquarters.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/330px-Epaheadquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/440px-Epaheadquarters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1389" data-file-height="941" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Epaheadquarters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
EPA headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiscal_mismanagement.2C_1983">Fiscal mismanagement, 1983</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Fiscal mismanagement, 1983">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1982 Congress charged that the EPA had mishandled the $1.6 billion program to clean up hazardous waste dumps <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> and demanded records from EPA director <a href="/wiki/Anne_Gorsuch_Burford" title="Anne Gorsuch Burford">Anne M. Gorsuch</a>. She refused and became the first agency director in U.S. history to be cited for <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_Congress" title="Contempt of Congress">contempt of Congress</a>. The EPA turned the documents over to Congress several months later, after the White House abandoned its court claim that the documents could not be subpoened by Congress because they were covered by <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a>. At that point, Gorsuch resigned her post, citing pressures caused by the media and the congressional investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-toledoblade_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toledoblade-76">[76]</a></sup> Critics charged that the EPA was in a shambles at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-spokanechronicle_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spokanechronicle-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gorsuch, appointed by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, resigned under fire in 1983. Gorsuch based her administration of the EPA on the <a href="/wiki/New_Federalism" title="New Federalism">New Federalism</a> approach of downsizing federal agencies by delegating their functions and services to the individual states.<sup id="cite_ref-epajournal_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epajournal-78">[78]</a></sup> She believed that the EPA was over-regulating business and that the agency was too large and not cost-effective. During her 22 months as agency head, she cut the budget of the EPA by 22%, reduced the number of cases filed against polluters, relaxed Clean Air Act regulations, and facilitated the spraying of restricted-use pesticides. She cut the total number of agency employees, and hired staff from the industries they were supposed to be regulating.<sup id="cite_ref-wapo_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">Environmentalists</a> contended that her policies were designed to placate polluters, and accused her of trying to dismantle the agency.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_pressure_and_scientific_integrity.2C_2001-present">Political pressure and scientific integrity, 2001-present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Political pressure and scientific integrity, 2001-present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> said that more than half of the nearly 1,600 EPA staff scientists who responded online to a detailed questionnaire reported they had experienced incidents of political interference in their work. The survey included chemists, toxicologists, engineers, geologists and experts in other fields of science. About 40% of the scientists reported that the interference had been more prevalent in the last five years than in previous years. The highest number of complaints came from scientists who were involved in determining the risks of cancer by chemicals used in food and other aspects of everyday life.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>EPA research has also been suppressed by career managers.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Supervisors at EPA's National Center for Environmental Assessment required several paragraphs to be deleted from a peer-reviewed journal article about EPA's integrated risk information system, which led two co-authors to have their names removed from the publication, and the corresponding author, Ching-Hung Hsu, to leave EPA "because of the draconian restrictions placed on publishing".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> EPA subjects employees who author scientific papers to <a href="/wiki/Prior_restraint" title="Prior restraint">prior restraint</a>, even if those papers are written on personal time.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>EPA employees have reported difficulty in conducting and reporting the results of studies on <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> due to industry<sup id="cite_ref-Urbina_03Mar2011_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urbina_03Mar2011-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPA_re_study_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_re_study-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chesapeake_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chesapeake-87">[87]</a></sup> and governmental pressure, and are concerned about the censorship of environmental reports.<sup id="cite_ref-Urbina_03Mar2011_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urbina_03Mar2011-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT_lobbying_docs_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_lobbying_docs-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT_Docs_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Docs-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> stated that the EPA violated federal law with covert propaganda on their social media platforms. The social media messaging that was used promoted materials supporting the <a href="/wiki/Waters_of_the_United_States_Regulatory_Overreach_Protection_Act_of_2014" title="Waters of the United States Regulatory Overreach Protection Act of 2014">Waters of the United States</a> rule, including materials that were designed to oppose legislative efforts to limit or block the rule.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hillview_reservoir_cost.2C_2005">Hillview reservoir cost, 2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Hillview reservoir cost, 2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, the EPA issued the Long Term 2 Enhanced Surface Water Treatment Rule (LT2), which requires covering open-air reservoirs containing finished drinking water, in order to reduce the incidence of disease caused by microorganisms in drinking water.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>To comply with the rule the EPA ordered that a cap be placed over the <a href="/wiki/Hillview_Reservoir" title="Hillview Reservoir">Hillview Reservoir</a>, where water bound for New York City receives its final disinfection before entering the pipelines serving the city.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> A number of city and state officials complained that the project was too costly and unnecessary.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_economy.2C_2005-2010">Fuel economy, 2005-2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Fuel economy, 2005-2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 2005, an EPA report showing that auto companies were using loopholes to produce less fuel-efficient cars was delayed. The report was supposed to be released the day before a controversial energy bill was passed and would have provided backup for those opposed to it, but the EPA delayed its release at the last minute .<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, the state of California sued the EPA for its refusal to allow California and 16 other states to raise fuel economy standards for new cars.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> EPA administrator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_L._Johnson" title="Stephen L. Johnson">Stephen L. Johnson</a> claimed that the EPA was working on its own standards, but the move has been widely considered an attempt to shield the auto industry from environmental regulation by setting lower standards at the federal level, which would then preempt state laws.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> California governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>, along with governors from 13 other states, stated that the EPA's actions ignored federal law, and that <i>existing</i> California standards (adopted by many states in addition to California) were almost twice as effective as the <i>proposed</i> federal standards.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> It was reported that Stephen Johnson ignored his own staff in making this decision.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the federal government had bailed out <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_crisis_of_2008%E2%80%932010" class="mw-redirect" title="Automotive industry crisis of 2008–2010">Automotive industry crisis of 2008–2010</a>, the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Equinox" title="Chevrolet Equinox">Chevrolet Equinox</a> was released with an EPA fuel economy rating abnormally higher than its competitors. Independent road tests<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> found that the vehicle did not out-perform its competitors, which had much lower fuel economy ratings. Later road tests found better, but inconclusive, results.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_emissions.2C_2005">Mercury emissions, 2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Mercury emissions, 2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2005, nine states (California, New York, New Jersey, New Hampshire, Massachusetts, Maine, Connecticut, New Mexico and Vermont) sued the EPA. The EPA's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Inspector_General_(United_States)" title="Office of Inspector General (United States)">Inspector General</a> had determined that the EPA's regulation of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> emissions did not follow the Clean Air Act, and that the regulations were influenced by top political appointees.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The EPA had suppressed a study it commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> which contradicted its position on mercury controls.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The suit alleged that the EPA's rule exempting coal-fired power plants from "maximum available control technology" was illegal, and additionally charged that the EPA's system of <a href="/wiki/Cap-and-trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Cap-and-trade">cap-and-trade</a> to lower average mercury levels would allow power plants to forego reducing mercury emissions, which they objected would lead to dangerous local hotspots of mercury contamination even if average levels declined.<sup id="cite_ref-Bustillo2005_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bustillo2005-110">[110]</a></sup> Several states also began to enact their own mercury emission regulations. Illinois's proposed rule would have reduced mercury emissions from power plants by an average of 90% by 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In 2008—by which point a total of fourteen states had joined the suit—the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia ruled that the EPA regulations violated the Clean Air Act.<sup id="cite_ref-Baltimore2008_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baltimore2008-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response, EPA announced plans to propose such standards to replace the vacated Clean Air Mercury Rule, and did so on March 16, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_MATS2011_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_MATS2011-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change.2C_2007-2010">Climate change, 2007-2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Climate change, 2007-2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinews-logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" width="40" height="22" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="759" data-file-height="415" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news: <i><b><a href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/EPA_proposes_using_Clean_Air_Act_to_fight_global_warming" class="extiw" title="wikinews:EPA proposes using Clean Air Act to fight global warming">EPA proposes using Clean Air Act to fight global warming</a></b></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In December 2007, EPA Administrator Stephen L. Johnson approved a draft of a document that declared that climate change imperiled the public welfare—a decision that would trigger the first national mandatory global-warming regulations. Associate Deputy Administrator Jason Burnett e-mailed the draft to the White House. White House aides—who had long resisted mandatory regulations as a way to address climate change—knew the gist of what Johnson's finding would be, Burnett said. They also knew that once they opened the attachment, it would become a public record, making it controversial and difficult to rescind. So they did not open it; rather, they called Johnson and asked him to take back the draft. Johnson rescinded the draft; in July 2008, he issued a new version which did not state that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> was danger to public welfare. Burnett resigned in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>A $3 million mapping study on <a href="/wiki/Climate_Change_Science_Program#Coastal_sensitivity_to_sea_level_rise_.28SAP_4.1.29" title="Climate Change Science Program"><i>sea level rise</i></a> was suppressed by EPA management during both the Bush and Obama Administrations, and managers changed a key interagency report to reflect the removal of the maps.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_King_Mine_waste_water_spill.2C_2015">Gold King Mine waste water spill, 2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Gold King Mine waste water spill, 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2015_Gold_King_Mine_waste_water_spill" title="2015 Gold King Mine waste water spill">2015 Gold King Mine waste water spill</a></div>
<p>On August 5, 2015, while examining the level of pollutants in the Gold King Mine,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> EPA contractors released over three million gallons of toxic <a href="/wiki/Waste_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste water">waste water</a>, including heavy metals such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> into Cement Creek, which flowed into the <a href="/wiki/Animas_River" title="Animas River">Animas River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-aug10spillfox_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aug10spillfox-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_Trump.2C_2017">President Trump, 2017</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: President Trump, 2017">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since taking office January 2017, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> has sought to reduce EPA employees´ amount of public interaction via social media.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> "The president has... barred [EPA employees] from awarding new contracts or grants as well." <sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> "Some career environmental regulators who conceded that they remained worried about what President Trump might do on policy matters—said such orders were not much different from those delivered by the Obama administration as it shifted policies from the departing White House of George W. Bush."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The Associated Press quoted employees as saying this was unusual. Trump has targeted 30 environmental rules for delayed implementation, "including updated pollution rulings for several states, renewable fuel standards and limits on the amount of formaldehyde that can leach from wood products".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2017, U.S. Representative <a href="/wiki/Matt_Gaetz" title="Matt Gaetz">Matt Gaetz</a> of Florida sponsored a bill,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> to abolish the EPA by 2018. According to Gaetz, "The American people are drowning in rules and regulation promulgated by unelected bureaucrats. And the Environmental Protection Agency has become an extraordinary offender." The bill was co-sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Massie" title="Thomas Massie">Thomas Massie</a> (R-Ky.), <a href="/wiki/Steven_Palazzo" title="Steven Palazzo">Steven Palazzo</a> (R-Ms.) and <a href="/wiki/Barry_Loudermilk" title="Barry Loudermilk">Barry Loudermilk</a> (R-Ga.).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
